    Discovery of a Potent Kelch-Like ECH-Associated Protein 1-Nuclear Factor Erythroid 2-Related Factor 2 (Keap1–Nrf2) Protein–Protein Interaction Inhibitor with Natural Proline Structure as a Cytoprotective Agent against Acetaminophen-Induced Hepatotoxicity

    Abstract

    The transcription factor Nrf2 is a key regulator of cytoprotective system, and enhancing Nrf2 activity can protect cells from various insults and threats. Directly disrupting Keap1–Nrf2 protein–protein interactions has been regarded as a promising way to activate Nrf2. We reported here the first identification of amino acids as preferred substituents to design potent Keap1–Nrf2 inhibitors. Comprehensive structure–activity analysis identified Pro as a preferred substituent, obtaining a potent inhibitor 35 with an IC50 of 43 nM in the competitive fluoresce polarization (FP) assay and a Kd value of 53.7 nM for Keap1 protein in the isothermal titration calorimetry (ITC) assay. The Pro analogue 35 exhibited tight and prolonged Keap1 binding in vitro and in cells, and treatment with 35 activated Nrf2-regulated cytoprotective response and antagonized acetaminophen-induced liver injury both in cellular and in vivo models. This work not only provides a useful tool to further explore the therapeutic potential of Keap1–Nrf2 inhibition but also enriches the diversity of chemical structures suitable for the Keap1–Nrf2 interface.

    Introduction

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    The human body is constantly exposed to reactive oxidants and electrophiles from both endogenous metabolic process and exogenous resources. These reactive insults not only directly damage the biological macromolecules including nuclear acids, protein, and lipids, but also induce the harmful oxidative stress.(1) However, proper amount of reactive oxygen species (ROS) and reactive nitrogen species (RNS) are essential in signal transduction, therefore a fine-tuned antioxidant system is critical for the human body.(2,3) Nuclear factor erythroid 2-related factor 2 (Nrf2), an antioxidant response element (ARE)-binding transcription factor, has been regarded as a key regulator of cellular defense system to antagonize oxidative and electrophile insults. By binding to ARE, Nrf2 induces the transcription of a battery of genes, of which products are involved in the glutathione-based and thioredoxin-based antioxidant systems and phase I, II, and III drug metabolizing enzymes.(4,5)
    Kelch-like ECH-associated protein 1 (Keap1) is the switch of Nrf2 activation in response to cellular microenvironment.(6) Keap1 acts as an adapter in the cullins3 (Cul3)-based E3 ligase, recognizing Nrf2 by its Kelch repeat domain, and interacting with Cul3 by its Broad-complex, Tramtrack, and Bric-a-Brac (BTB) domain.(7,8) Under normal conditions, Nrf2 is sequestered by Keap1 dimer and then ubiquitinated by Keap1–Cul3 E3 ligase, which leads to the degradation of Nrf2 by the proteasome. Under stress conditions, the reactive cysteine residues in Keap1 can be covalently modified by increased oxidants and electrophiles, resulting in the conformation changes of Keap1 that inactivate the Keap1–Cul3 E3 ligase.(8,9) Thus, the newly synthesized Nrf2 can get rid of Keap1-dependent repression, and the increasing level of free Nrf2 will be translocated into nucleus to induce the transcription of downstream genes.(10) The adequate and timely Nrf2 activation is important for cells to combat stress conditions, and the insufficient Nrf2 activity has been regarded as the pathogenic factors for multiple inflammatory and stress-related diseases.(11) Enhancing Nrf2 activity may have potential usages in various stress injury related diseases involved in neuronal, respiratory, hepatic, and gastrointestinal conditions.(12)
    Recently, directly and competitively disrupting Keap1–Nrf2 protein–protein interaction (PPI) has emerged as a promising way to enhance Nrf2 activity.(13,14) Compared with classic electrophilic Nrf2 activators, Keap1–Nrf2 PPI inhibitors can bind to Keap1 at a competitive and selective manner, which reduces uncertain safety risk.(15) The interaction of Keap1 and Nrf2 follows a 2:1 stoichiometric model, namely one Nrf2 protein binds to Keap1 dimer through the high affinity ETGE motif and the low affinity DLG motif.(16) The two different motifs in Nrf2 occupy a similar cavity at the center of the β-propeller fold in Keap1 Kelch domain but show distinct binding behaviors.(17) Because of its high binding potency and short sequence length, the ETGE containing motif provides a good starting point for research of Keap1–Nrf2 inhibitors. A cell-penetrant ETGE-derived peptide conjugated with trans-activating transcriptional activator (TAT) sequence confirmed the Nrf2 activation effects,(18) and then a number of peptide inhibitors were reported to explore the interface of Keap1–Nrf2 PPI,(19−26) revealing the five subpockets model of the Keap1 cavity.(27,28) To date, a lot of small molecule Keap1–Nrf2 inhibitors have been reported,(29−38) and some of the inhibitors have achieved high potency.(39−44)
    We report here the identification of natural amino acids as preferred fragments for Keap1 binding and the discovery of a potent but low molecular weight Keap1–Nrf2 inhibitor containing Pro structure. The analysis of previously reported potent Keap1–Nrf2 inhibitors reveals the possibility of natural α-amino acids in occupying the polar pocket in Keap1. By incorporating α-amino acid substituents into the naphthalene sulfonamide scaffold, we obtained a series of inhibitors with diverse chemical structures. Structure–activity relationship (SAR) study identified the Pro and Phe as two preferred substituents, and the proline derived compound 35 showed tight binding to Keap1 protein both in vitro and in cells, potent Nrf2 activation effects in cellular assay, and effective cytoprotective effects against acetaminophen (N-acetyl-para-aminophenol [APAP])-induced injury both in vitro and in vivo. This research not only obtains a potent Keap1–Nrf2 inhibitor with the Pro substituent but also shows therapeutic potential of Keap1–Nrf2 inhibitors against APAP-induced hepatic injury.

    Results

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To

    Integrating Amino Acid Substituents to Design Keap1–Nrf2 Inhibitors

    It is well-known that PPI targets are the high hanging fruit for drug discovery. PPI interfaces are critical with chemical structure and difficult for design of potent ligands. In Keap1–Nrf2 PPI, the Nrf2 binding site in the Keap1 Kelch domain is a well-defined cavity containing five subpockets (Figure 1A,B). In the previous work, we introduced N-acetic acid groups that mimic Glu79 and Glu82 of Nrf2 ETGE motif to the naphthalene–disulfonamide scaffold (1), obtaining a symmetric compound (2) that can occupy all five subpockets (Figure 1C). Further SAR and pharmacology studies showed that this series of compounds exhibits strong binding affinity with a Keap1 Kelch domain and effective Nrf2 activation effects both in vitro and in vivo.(39−41,45) However, the high polar and symmetric structure of this compound series can result in poor physicochemical and drug-like properties and therefore needs to be further optimized. Reinspection of N-acetic acid substituted sulfonamide scaffold gave the thought that the natural amino acids might be potential building blocks for discovery of novel inhibitor because the N-acetic acid moiety can be regarded as the substituted Gly. The structure diversity of amino acids could benefit the identification of structure that could recapitulate the binding pattern with Keap1. We first examined the toleration of Keap1 cavity for the amino acid side chain. The cocrystal structure of compound 3, the amide analogue of 2, with Kelch domain elucidates the binding mode of this chemotype with Keap1.(46) As shown in Figure 1D, the methylene in the N-acetamide group locating at the P1 subpocket points to the P4 subpocket and the space around the methylene can accommodate the substituted side chain. Moreover, the crystal structure shows that the symmetric compound does not form strong interactions with Tyr525 in the P4 subpocket, while in the case of the most potent inhibitor, compound 4, reported by GlaxoSmithKline Pharmaceuticals,(42) forms a stacking interaction with Tyr525 (Figure 1E). By replacing one N-acetic acid substituted sulfonamide, amino acid building blocks with various side chain structures can facilitate the exploration of the P4 subpocket.

    SAR of Aliphatic Amino Acid Substituents

    The aliphatic amino acids with flexible side chains were synthesized to probe the possibility of amino acid in inhibitor design (Schemes 1 and 2). As shown in Table 1, the Ala derivative 5 and the Val analogue 7 exhibited similar activity. The Ile analogue 9 is much more potent than the Leu analogue 11, indicating that the terminal methyl branch significantly impaired the Keap1 binding. Compound 17 with the side chain of t-Bu also showed poor activity. The Met analogue 13 and its oxidative analogue 15 showed nearly equal activity to 9. In the compounds without the N-acetic acid fragment, only the Ile analogue 10 and the Met analogue 14 showed detectable activities. Interestingly, the Met analogues 13 and 14 showed almost the same activity, indicating the extra acidic moiety is unnecessary in this chemotype. In addition, 19 and 21 with O-tert-butyl protected Ser and Thr substituents showed obvious inhibitory activity, indicating the tert-butoxy side-chain is favorable for Keap1 binding.
    Table 1. Inhibitory Activity of Compounds Containing Aliphatic Amino Acid Substituents

    SAR of Polar Amino Acid Substituents

    The polar amino acids were introduced into the scaffold to obtain analogues in Table 2. The Ser analogue 23 did not show detectable activity (Scheme 3). Together with the active Ala analogue, it indicated the unfitness of the hydroxy group for this site. We also tried to get the Thr analogues. Unfortunately, the final products were not stable, and we only obtained the hydroxy-protected compounds which have been discussed before. Compared with Ala analogue 5, both Asp and Glu analogues 25 and 27 showed the enhancement in activity, suggesting that the carboxy-containing side chain might make extra polar interactions with Keap1.
    Table 2. Inhibitory Activity of Compounds Containing Polar Amino Acid Substituents

    SAR of Ring-Containing Amino Acid Substituents

    Then, compounds bearing ring-containing amino acid substituents in Table 3 were synthesized. As shown in Figure 1E, the Tyr525 in P4 is a potential site for small molecule binding. The aromatic amino acids are potential substituents for interacting with Tyr525. As expected, the Phe analogue 29 got an IC50 of 300 nM, while the Tyr analogue 31 was much less potent than 29. The binding mode from docking study gave the consistent prediction in which the Phe analogue 29 can form a π–π stacking interaction with Tyr525 at an appropriate manner and the other part of the molecule can interact with Keap1 in a similar way as the lead compound 2 (Figure 2A). The Trp analogue 33 showed comparable activity with 29, indicating the volume tolerance in this site. After the research of aromatic amino acids, the Pro analogue 35 was synthesized (Scheme 4) and the biological evaluation gave the dramatic result that this compound showed nearly equal activity (IC50 = 43 nM) with the lead compound 2. The concise structure (MW = 484.5) and potent activity of 35 indicated that the Pro substituent might provide a unique and fine-tuned manner to interact with P1 and P4 simultaneously. The docking study gave some insights into the binding mode, in which the five-membered ring can not only locate the carboxy group into the P1 cavity to form vital polar interaction with Arg483 of Keap1 but also form hydrophobic interaction with the benzene ring of Tyr525 (Figure 2B). Interestingly, another Keap1–Nrf2 PPI inhibitor RA839 contains the pyrrolidine-3-carboxylic acid fragment which is quite similar to the Pro. This compound, containing only one carboxyl group, showed a Kd of 6 μM to Keap1 in an isothermal titration calorimetry (ITC) assay and an IC50 of 0.14 μM in a fluorescence polarization (FP) assay, which further stressed that the pyrrolidine ring is preferable for the Keap1–Nrf2 inhibitors.(33)
    Table 3. Inhibitory Activity of Compounds Containing Amino Acid Substituents with Ring Structures
    To further confirm the binding mode, the d-Pro was used to construct an enantiomer (37) for control (Scheme 4). This compound exhibited huge loss in inhibitory activity, which supported the proposed binding mode of 35. Then we tried to further optimize the benzene sulfonamide fragment of this scaffold. Another three favorable substituents, 4-methyl, 2,4,6-trimethyl, and 4-acetamido, were used to try to optimize the structure (Table 4). However, the results showed that the original 4-methoxyl is optimal. In this series, all analogues without the N-acetic acid moiety showed poor inhibitory activity, indicating that the acidic group occupying P2 subpocket is important for the Keap1 binding of this chemotype.

    Pro Analogue 35 Exhibited Tight Keap1 Binding Both in Vitro and in Cells

    Compound 35 showed impressive inhibitory activity in disrupting the interaction of Keap1 and Nrf2 ETGE peptide. To validate the high Keap1 binding affinity and analyze the Keap1 binding characters of the Pro analogue 35, the Keap1 binding properties of 35 were investigated from the perspective of both binding thermodynamics and kinetics. Thermodynamic parameters of Keap1–35 interaction were determined by the ITC, and it gave a Kd of 53.7 nM (Figure 3A), similar with the optimal symmetric diacetic compounds.(45) Noticeably, 35 showed a putative enthalpy-driven binding process. The heat of the exothermic reactions was as much as 28.6 kcal/mol, indicating the formation of numerous noncovalent interactions. Certainly, the entropy loss in this process was also huge, which provided tips for the further structure optimization. Then we evaluated the binding kinetics of 35 to Keap1 to examine the effects of Pro substituent on the association and dissociation process. The biolayer interferometry (BLI) technique was used to monitor the whole process of binding, and in line with the ITC data, the BLI experiment gave a Kd of 28.5 nM (Figure 3B). Moreover, the dissociation curves showed that 35 is a long acting compound with prolonged residence time. Inspired by these in vitro data, the cellular Keap1 binding behavior of 35 was examined by cellular thermal shift assay (CESTA)(47) that can monitor target engagement by drugs in cells. As shown in Figure 3C, the addition of compound 35 could significantly enhance the thermostability of Keap1 in the Western blots. The protein thermal melting curves clearly showed that after treatment with compound 35, obvious shifts in the melting curves were detected, which strongly supported that compound 35 could bind to Keap1 in cells (Figure 3D).

    Pro Analogue 35 Effectively Activated Nrf2-Regulated Cytoprotective Defense System

    After fully confirming the strong Keap1 binding potency of 35, the cellular Nrf2 activation activity was examined. HepG2-ARE-C8 cells that had been stably transfected with an ARE-controlled luciferase reporter were treated with different concentrations (0.01, 0.1, 1, 5, 10, and 20 μM) of compounds. As shown in Table 5, compound 35 showed a clear concentration-dependent upregulation of ARE-controlled luciferase activity and induced the luciferase activity by 10.6-fold over the DMSO control at a maximal concentration of 20 μM. We next evaluated the potency of compound 35 to upregulate Nrf2-dependent gene expression in normal hepatic L02 cells. No apparent cytotoxicity was observed toward L02 cells with compound 35 at concentrations up to 100 μM (Supporting Information, Figure S1). Compound 35 increased the mRNA level of Nrf2-regulated genes, NEFL2L (encoding Nrf2 protein), HO-1, NQO-1, and GCLM, in L02 cells at a concentration-dependent manner (Figure 4A). At a time-course study, Nrf2 activation effects were observed after 8 h of compound 35 treatment and reached the maximal effects at 24 h (Figure 4B). Western blotting experiments gave the consistent results that compound 35 could concentration-dependently up-regulate the protein level of Nrf2, heme oxygen-1(HO-1), NAD(P)H dehydrogenase (quinone) 1 (NQO-1), and glutamate–cysteine ligase regulatory subunit (GCLM) in L02 cells (Figure 4C,D).
    Table 5. Induction of ARE Activity in HepG2-ARE-C8 Cellsa
     0.01 μM0.1 μM1 μM5 μM10 μM20 μM
    351.02 ± 0.231.04 ± 0.291.60 ± 0.463.68 ± 0.876.94 ± 0.3910.68 ± 1.05

    Values shown are mean ± SD (n = 3, 12 h of treatment). Cells were exposed to compounds tested for their ability to increase levels of relative luciferase units.

    By using Nrf2 siRNA, we further examined whether the transcriptional activation effects of 35 is dependent on Nrf2. As shown in Figure 5, compound 35 increased mRNA expression and protein products of these genes in L02 cells transfected with the nontargeting siRNA, while knocking down Nrf2 by siRNA nearly abolished the activity of compound 35.

    Pro Analogue 35 Prevented APAP-Induced Hepatotoxicity in L02 Cells

    Acetaminophen, the most widely used analgesic, causes severe hepatic necrosis leading to acute liver failure (ALF) after intentional overdoses.(48) Large dose of APAP can induce the oxidative stress, including the elevation of ROS, the exhaustion of glutathione (GSH), and the imbalance of cellular oxidative state, which can be recovered by Nrf2 activation.(49,50) Thus, we examined the effects of 35 on APAP-induced hepatotoxicity in both cellular and in vivo models. Hepatic L02 cells were pretreated with compound or DMSO for approximately 12 h before addition of 7.5 mM APAP. As shown in Figure 6A, APAP exposure induced severe cell viability inhibition, while pretreatment with 35 showed significant cytoprotective effects even at 1 μM. Nrf2 gene silencing abolished cytoprotective effects of 35 against APAP (Figure 6B). Flow cytometric analysis showed that exposure to 7.5 mM APAP mainly induced the late apoptosis of L02 cells and caused the apoptosis rate to 48.8% in L02 cells. Pretreatment with 10 μM of 35 significantly decreased the APAP-induced apoptosis level to 18.3% (Figure 6C).
    Besides the direct cytotoxicity effects, APAP also can induce the inflammatory conditions. As shown in Figure 7A–C, L02 cells that were exposed to APAP showed an obvious increase of inflammatory factors, including IL-1β, IL-6, and TNF-α, while pretreatment with 35 significantly inhibited the production of these inflammatory factors. Then the oxidative-related markers including MDA and GSH/GSSG ratio were assessed, and 35 down-regulated MDA and up-regulated the GSH/GSSG ratio, indicating that 35 can relieve APAP-induced oxidative stress (Figure 7D,E). Alkaline phosphatase (ALP) and alanine transaminase (ALT) are two important markers, which will be elevated at hepatic injury conditions. Pretreatment with 35 significantly suppressed APAP-induced up-regulation of ALP and ALT, which strongly supported the cytoprotective effects of 35 on APAP-treated L02 cells(Figure 7F,G).

    Pro Analogue 35 Prevented APAP-Induced Hepatotoxicity in Vivo

    Then APAP challenged mice model was used to evaluate in vivo protective effects of compound 35. To confirm the engagement of the Nrf2 pathway by compound 35 in vivo, the protein expression of Nrf2, HO-1, NQO1, and GCLM in the liver tissue were examined by immunohistochemical staining. As shown in Figure 8A, treatment with 10 mg/kg 35 induced the obvious elevation of the four proteins, and the high dose of 35 (40 mg/kg) showed more potent effects. In the subsequent analysis, the antioxidants effects of 35 were detected, and the quantitative results showed that treatment with 35 significantly decreased ROS level, MDA and MPO activity. and up-regulated GSH/GSSG ratio in liver tissue, which confirmed the in vivo antioxidant activity of 35 (Figure 8B–E).
    We next examined the effects of 35 on the pathological symptoms in the APAP-induced mouse model of acute liver injury. The H&E staining (Figure 9A) showed that treatment with APAP (400 mg/kg) induced severe liver damage, indicated by intrahepatic hemorrhage, lymphocytes infiltration, and the destruction of liver structure. Treatment with 35 significantly ameliorated such phenomenon. The TUNEL staining was used to monitor the hepatic cell apoptosis. As shown in Figure 9B, overdose of APAP-induced the apoptosis of a mass of hepatic cells, while treatment with 35 significantly reduced the number of apoptotic cells. Moreover, compound 35 also decreased the protein level of α-SMA, a marker for hepatic injury and fibrosis (Figure 9C). Likewise, APAP induced apparent hepatotoxicity as reflected by high necrosis grade, while these injuries were alleviated by 35, as reflected by the low histological disease scores (Figure 9D). ALT, ALP, and total bilirubin (TBIL) are important serum biomarkers for hepatic injury, which will be released into the circulation system under hepatic injury conditions. Compound 35 also significantly reduced these biomarkers at a dose of 10 mg/kg (Figure 9E–G). We also evaluated several representative inflammatory factors including IL-1β, IL-6, and TNF-α, to indicate the anti-inflammation effects of compound 35, and the results clearly showed that 10 mg/kg dose of compound 35 significantly relieved the APAP-induced inflammatory conditions (Figure 9H–J).

    Chemistry

    The synthesis of compound 5–14, 17–22, 25–30, 33, and 34 are shown in Scheme 1. Peroxidation of commercially available 1-nitronaphthalene afforded 4-nitro-1-naphthol (42). The phenol group of 42 was then reacted with Tf2O to give compound 43, which was coupled to a variety of amino acids to afford compounds 44a–l. The nitro group of 44a–l was reduced by hydrogen and Pd/C, and the subsequent condensation with 4-methoxybenzenesulfonyl chloride gave compounds 45a–l, respectively. Compounds 46a–l were obtained by nucleophilic substitution of NH by bromoacetate in the presence of K2CO3 in DMF. Hydrolysis of the ester groups of compounds 46a–l yielded the target compounds 5, 7, 9, 11, 13, 17, 19, 21, 25, 27, 29, and 33. Alternatively, hydrolysis of the ester groups of compounds 45a–l gave the target compounds 6, 8, 10, 12, 14, 18, 20, 22, 26, 28, 30, and 34 with monocarboxylic acid.
    The syntheses of compound 15 and 16 are shown in Scheme 2. Compound 45e was allowed to react with m-chloroperoxybenzoic acid in dichloromethane to give compound 47. Hydrolysis of the ester group of 47 yielded the final compound 16. Nucleophilic substitution of NH of compound 47 by bromoacetate afforded compound 48, and the subsequent hydrolysis of the ester groups produced the final compound 15.
    The syntheses of compound 23, 24, 31, and 32 are shown in Scheme 3. The coupling reaction of compound 43 with different amino acids afforded compounds 49a,b. After reduction and the subsequent condensation with 4-methoxybenzenesulfonyl chloride, compounds 49a and 49b were transformed into 50a and 50b, respectively. Deprotection of the benzyl group with Pd/C in THF/CH3OH solution at 50 °C produced compound 51a. Nucleophilic substitution of NH of compound 51a by bromoacetate afforded compound 52, and the subsequent hydrolysis of the ester groups produced the final compound 23. Alternatively, hydrolysis of the ester groups of compounds 51a gave the target compound 24 with monocarboxylic acid. Compound 32 was synthesized according to the similar procedure of 24. Compound 53 was afforded by nucleophilic substitution of NH of compound 50b by bromoacetate. Deprotection of the benzyl group of 50b with Pd/C in THF/CH3OH solution at 50 °C produced compound 54, and the subsequent hydrolysis of the ester groups gave the final compound 31.
    The syntheses of compound 35–41 are shown in Scheme 4. Compound 43 was allowed to couple with methyl l/d-prolinate hydrochloride to afford compounds 55a and 55b, respectively. After reduction and the subsequent condensation with substituted benzenesulfonyl chloride, compounds 55a and 55b were transformed to 56a–e. Hydrolysis of the ester groups of compounds 56a and 56b gave the final compounds 36 and 38, respectively, with monocarboxylic acid. Nucleophilic substitution of NH of compounds 56a–e by bromoacetate afforded compounds 57a–e, and the subsequent hydrolysis of the ester groups yielded the final compounds 35, 37, and 39–41.

    Discussion and Conclusions

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    In this work, we identified amino acids as the preferred substituents for Keap1 binding. By replacing the N-acetic acid substituted benzenesulfonamide fragment with amino acid structure, we comprehensively analyzed the SAR of the amino acids in Keap1–Nrf2 inhibitors and obtained Phe and Pro analogues as potent Keap1–Nrf2 inhibitors. Remarkably, the Pro analogue 35 with concise chemical structure showed potent Keap1 binding affinity both in vitro and in cells and exhibited protective effects against APAP-induced hepatic injury both in vitro and in vivo.
    Nrf2 activation has been regarded as a hopeful strategy in stress and inflammatory related diseases for the key role of Nrf2 in oxidative stress regulation and cell defense system. Directly disrupting Keap1–Nrf2 PPI is increasingly recognized as a targeted and specific way to develop therapeutic agents of enhancing Nrf2 activity. However, the chemodiversity of potent Keap1–Nrf2 inhibitors is still limited. By analysis of the symmetric potent inhibitors reported previously, we suggested that the amino acids may match the P1 and P4 subpocket, and a series of compounds containing diverse amino acid substituents were synthesized and evaluated. The most dramatic substituent is Pro, which not only points into P1 cavity mimicking Glu79 of Nrf2 but also forms favor hydrophobic interactions with the P4 subpocket. The Pro analogue 35 with MW of only 484.5, showed potent Keap1 binding affinity and prolonged resident time with Keap1, further confirming the feasibility of a fine-tuned small molecule to effectively disrupt Keap1–Nrf2 PPI. The Phe analogue also exhibited potent PPI inhibition activity and the Phe substituent might form polar interactions in P1 subpocket and π–π stacking with Tyr525. Compound 35 is also a promising hit for further design of potent Keap1–Nrf2 inhibitors.
    Hepatic injury induced by overdose of drugs, especially APAP and mistaken poisoning, is the main reason that leads to ALF. In our study, compound 35 can activate Nrf2-regulated cytoprotective system at a Nrf2-dependent manner in hepatic L02 cells and liver tissue. Treatment with 35 can relieve the oxidative stress, which was confirmed by the decrease of ROS and increase of GSH. Activation of Nrf2-regulated cytoprotective system can protect cells from APAP-induced cell viability inhibition and cell death. The decrease of hepatic injury markers, including ALT, ALP, and TBIL also proved that 35 could effectively relieve the APAP-induced liver injury. Moreover, 35 also significantly suppressed APAP-induced inflammatory conditions. Collectively, these results confirmed that the Keap1–Nrf2 inhibitor 35 could antagonize APAP-induced liver injury, which provided a potential usage of Keap1–Nrf2 inhibitory agents.
    In this study, we identified amino acids as preferred substituents for Keap1 binding, obtained a Pro analogue 35 as a potent Keap1–Nrf2 inhibitor with concise chemical structure and proved the therapeutic potential of Keap1–Nrf2 inhibitor 35 in APAP-induced liver injury. We hope these results can promote the further development of Keap1–Nrf2 inhibitors as clinically used drugs.

    Experimental Section

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To

    1. Chemistry

    The synthesis of test compounds is highlighted in Schemes 1–4. Reactions were monitored by thin-layer chromatography (TLC) on 0.25 mm silica gel plates (GF254) and visualized under UV light. Melting points were determined with a Melt-Temp II apparatus. The 1H NMR and 13C NMR spectra were measured on a Bruker AV-300 instrument using deuterated solvents with tetramethylsilane (TMS) as the internal standard. ESI-mass and high-resolution mass spectra (HRMS) were recorded on a Water Q-Tof micro mass spectrometer. The purity (≥95%) of the compounds was verified by HPLC performed on an Agilent C18 (4.6 mm × 150 mm, 3.5 μm) column using a mixture of solvent methanol/water 80:20 with 1‰ TFA at the flow rate of 0.5 mL/min and peak detection at 254 nm.

    (4-((N-(Carboxymethyl)-4-methoxyphenyl)sulfonamido)naphthalen-1-yl)-l-alanine (5)

    To a solution of 46a (100.0 mg, 206.0 μmol) in MeOH/H2O (5 mL/5 mL) was added LiOH (173.0 mg, 4.1 mmol). After 10 h stirring at room temperature, the reaction mixture was then diluted in 30 mL of water and quenched with 2 M hydrochloric acid to pH 5. The crude product was obtained through filtration. Recrystallization from ethyl acetate/n-hexane gave the compound 5 as a white solid, yield 77%; mp >250 °C. 1H NMR (300 MHz, DMSO-d6) δ 12.69 (br, 2H), 8.29 (d, J = 7.8 Hz, 1H), 8.05–7.89 (m, 1H), 7.63–7.56 (m, 2H), 7.45–7.42 (m, 2H), 7.05 (dd, J = 1.89, 1.89 Hz, 2H), 6.93–6.91 (m, 1H), 6.29–6.18 (m, 1H), 4.37 (d, J = 5.22 Hz, 1H), 4.23 (d, J = 13.92 Hz, 1H), 4.13 (m, 1H), 3.80 (s, 3H), 1.52 (dd, J = 6.48 Hz, 12.72 Hz, 3H). HRMS (ESI): found 459.1222 (C22H22N2O7S, [M + H]+, requires 459.1221). HPLC (80:20 methanol:water with 1‰ TFA): tR = 3.56 min, 96.3%.

    2. Molecular Docking

    The Ligandfit docking tool in Discovery Studio, which has been validated for this target previously,(41) was used to predict the binding mode of the small molecular inhibitor. The docking site was derived from the position of the small molecular ligand cocrystallized in the binding site of Keap1 (PDB 4XMB).

    3. Biology

    ITC (Isothermal Titration Calorimetry) Assay

    The Isothermal titration calorimetry (ITC) was performed at 25 °C with the ITC200 system (MicroCal). The Keap1 Kelch domain was lyophilized. Both the protein sample and the compound were dissolved in 10 mM HEPES buffer (pH 7.4). Then 2 μL aliquots of 0.05 mM compound were injected 19 times at 2.5 min intervals from a stirring syringe (750 rpm) into the sample cell containing 220 μL of 0.005 mM Keap1 Kelch domain. The data were analyzed with the computer program Origin, version 7.0, supplied by MicroCal.

    BLI (Biolayer Interferometry) Assay

    The Keap1 Kelch domain was biotinylated in a buffer of 20 mM HEPES, pH 7.0. The interaction between the ligand and the Keap1 Kelch domain was determined by biolayer interferometry using an Octet Red 96 instrument (FortéBio Inc.). Super Streptavidin Biosensors tips (FortéBio, Inc., Menlo Park, CA) were prewetted with buffer (FortéBio) to establish a baseline before protein immobilization. Then the biotinylated protein target was immobilized onto Super Streptavidin Biosensors. All of the binding data were collected at 30 °C. The experiments comprised five steps: (1) baseline acquisition, (2) protein loading onto the sensor, (3) second baseline acquisition, (4) association of the ligand for the measurement of kon, and (5) dissociation of the ligands for the measurement of koff. Four concentrations of various ligands were used for detection. The association and dissociation plot and kinetic constants were obtained with FortéBio data analysis software. Equilibrium dissociation constants (Kd) were calculated by the ratio of koff to kon.

    Cell Culture Conditions

    HepG2 cells stably transfected with a luciferase reporter (HepG2-ARE-C8) were kindly provided by Professor Dr. A. N. Tony Kong (Rutgers University, Piscataway, NJ) and Prof. Rong Hu (China Pharmaceutical University, Nanjing). Human L02 cells were purchased from Cell Bank of Shanghai Institute of Biochemistry and Cell Biology, Shanghai Institutes for Biological Sciences, Chinese Academy of Sciences. Cells were maintained in modified RPMI-1640 medium (GiBco, Invitrogen Corp., USA) with 10% fetal bovine serum (FBS) (Gibco, Invitrogen Corp., USA) and penicillin/streptomycin in a 37 °C incubator with 5% CO2.

    RNA Extraction and qRT-PCR Analysis

    The experimental procedure of quantitative real-time RT-PCR was previously reported. Total RNA of L02 cells was extracted from the treated cells using TRIzol reagent (Invitrogen). Then the RNA was converted to cDNA by reverse transcriptase (PrimeScript RT reagent kit) according to the manufacturer’s instructions. Quantitative real-time RT-PCR analysis of Nrf2, HO-1, NQO1, and GCLM were performed by using the StepOne System Fast real-time PCR system (Applied Biosystems). The values are expressed as the fold of the control. All genes’ mRNA expression was normalized against β-actin expression.

    Western Blot Analysis

    Anti-Nrf2 (ab62352) was purchased from Abcam (Abcam, UK). Anti-HO-1 (no. 5853S) were bought from Cell Signaling Technology (USA). Anti-NQO1 (sc-271116) and anti-GCLM (sc-22755) antibodies were purchased from Santa Cruz Biotechnology (Santa Cruz, CA, USA). Anti-Keap1 antibody (10503-2-AP) was bought from Proteintech Group, Inc. Anti-β-action (AP0060) was purchased from Bioworld (Bioworld, USA). Isolation of cell fractions and Western blotting were performed as detailed previously.(45) Briefly, the extracts were separated by SDS-PAGE and then electrotransferred to PVDF membranes (PerkinElmer, Northwalk, CT, USA). Membranes were blocked with 1% BSA for 1 h followed by incubation with a primary antibody at 4 °C overnight. Then they were washed and treated with a DyLight 800 labeled secondary antibody at 37 °C for 2 h. The membranes were screened through the odyssey infrared imaging System (LI-COR, Lincoln, Nebraska, USA).

    Detection of ROS Levels

    Human L02 cells were seeded in 6-well plates at the density of 70–80% confluence per well for overnight incubation. Then the cells were treated with compound for indicated time. After treatment, cells were washed once with 2 mL of 10% PBS and stained with 10 μM cH2DCF-DA (S0033, Reactive Oxygen Species Assay Kit, Beyotime, China) in the dark at 37 °C for 20 min in 1640 medium free with FBS. Analysis was done with a fluorescence microscope (OLYMPUS DP72, Japan) equipped with a U-RFL-T power supply.

    Flow Cytometric Detection of Apoptosis

    L02 cells in logarithmic phase in a 6-well tissue culture plate were treated with test samples for indicated time. Then they were harvested, washed, and resuspended with PBS. Apoptotic cells were determined with Muse Annexin V Dead Cell Kit (lot no.15-0610) according to the manufacturer’s protocol. Apoptosis was analyzed by a Muse cell analyzer (EMD Millipore Corporation, Hayward, CA).

    Acetaminophen-Induced Mouse Acute Liver Injury Model

    Animal studies were conducted according to protocols approved by Institutional Animal Care and Use Committee of China Pharmaceutical University. All animals were appropriately used in a scientifically valid and ethical manner. Female C57BL/6 mice, 6–8 weeks of age weighing 18–20 g, were acclimatized under a 12 h light/dark cycle at 22 °C and 60% humidity for 2 days before the experiments and fed with a standard laboratory rodent diet and water. Mice were given free access to diet and water during the course of experiments.
    Female C57BL/6 mice were randomly divided into four groups (n = 10): control group, APAP (Sigma-Aldrich, St. Louis) group (400 mg/kg), APAP (400 mg/kg) + 35 (10 mg/kg) group, and APAP (400 mg/kg) + 35 (40 mg/kg) group. APAP and compound 35 were dissolved in warm saline prior to administration. Animals in control group received a single IP injection containing 500 μL of saline. Two hours after the injection, a starvation period started and ended after 22 h. For the APAP dosing regimen, mice were pretreated with 35 (ip, in 500 μL of saline) for 2 h, then followed by a 22 h starvation period. The starvation was ended and APAP (400 mg/kg, ip, in 500 μL of saline) was administered. Animals were sacrificed 6 h later. For serum inflammatory cytokines and alanine aminotransferase (ALT) measurements, blood was drawn by cardiac puncture, and for histological analyses, livers were fixed in formalin.

      Sies, H.; Berndt, C.; Jones, D. P. Oxidative Stress. Annu. Rev. Biochem. 2017, 86, 715– 748,  DOI: 10.1146/annurev-biochem-061516-045037

      1
      Oxidative Stress
      Sies, Helmut; Berndt, Carsten; Jones, Dean P.
      Annual Review of Biochemistry (2017), 86 (), 715-748CODEN: ARBOAW; ISSN:0066-4154. (Annual Reviews)
      Oxidative stress is two sided: Whereas excessive oxidant challenge causes damage to biomols., maintenance of a physiol. level of oxidant challenge, termed oxidative eustress, is essential for governing life processes through redox signaling. Recent interest has focused on the intricate ways by which redox signaling integrates these converse properties. Redox balance is maintained by prevention, interception, and repair, and concomitantly the regulatory potential of mol. thiol-driven master switches such as Nrf2/Keap1 or NF-κB/IκB is used for system-wide oxidative stress response. Nonradical species such as hydrogen peroxide (H2O2) or singlet mol. oxygen, rather than free-radical species, perform major second messenger functions. Chemokine-controlled NADPH oxidases and metabolically controlled mitochondrial sources of H2O2 as well as glutathione- and thioredoxin-related pathways, with powerful enzymic back-up systems, are responsible for fine-tuning physiol. redox signaling. This makes for a rich research field spanning from biochem. and cell biol. into nutritional sciences, environmental medicine, and mol. knowledge-based redox medicine.

      Nrf2-Keap1 signaling in oxidative and reductive stress
      Bellezza, Ilaria; Giambanco, Ileana; Minelli, Alba; Donato, Rosario
      Biochimica et Biophysica Acta, Molecular Cell Research (2018), 1865 (5), 721-733CODEN: BBAMCO; ISSN:0167-4889. (Elsevier B.V.)
      Nrf2 and its endogenous inhibitor, Keap1, function as a ubiquitous, evolutionarily conserved intracellular defense mechanism to counteract oxidative stress. Sequestered by cytoplasmic Keap1 and targeted to proteasomal degrdn. in basal conditions, in case of oxidative stress Nrf2 detaches from Keap1 and translocates to the nucleus, where it heterodimerizes with one of the small Maf proteins. The heterodimers recognize the AREs, that are enhancer sequences present in the regulatory regions of Nrf2 target genes, essential for the recruitment of key factors for transcription. In the present review we briefly introduce the Nrf2-Keap1 system and describe Nrf2 functions, illustrate the Nrf2-NF-κB cross-talk, and highlight the effects of the Nrf2-Keap1 system in the physiol. and pathophysiol. of striated muscle tissue taking into account its role(s) in oxidative stress and reductive stress.

      Absolute amounts and status of the Nrf2-keap1-cul3 complex within cells
      Iso, Tatsuro; Suzuki, Takafumi; Baird, Liam; Yamamoto, Masayuki
      Molecular and Cellular Biology (2016), 36 (24), 3100-3112CODEN: MCEBD4; ISSN:1098-5549. (American Society for Microbiology)
      The transcription factor Nrf2 (NF-E2-related-factor 2) is essential for the oxidative and electrophilic stress responses. Keap1 (Kelch-like-ECH-assocd.-protein 1), an adaptor for a cullin-3 (Cul3)-based ubiquitin ligase, regulates Nrf2 activity through proteasomal degrdn., and acts as a sensor for oxidative and electrophilic stresses. The Keap1-Cul3 complex is a crit. regulator of the cellular Nrf2 level, and yet quant. information regarding their endogenous intracellular concns. in homeostatic conditions and during stress responses is unknown. We analyzed the abs. amts. of the Nrf2, Keap1, and Cul3 proteins in five murine cell lines by comparison with serial dilns. of purified recombinant protein stds. in combination with quant. immunoblot analyses. In the basal state, the amt. of Nrf2 was maintained at lower levels than those of Keap1 and Cul3 proteins, whereas the electrophilic agent diethylmaleate dramatically increased Nrf2 to a level greater than that of Keap1 and Cul3, resulting in the accumulation of Nrf2 in the nucleus. In contrast, Keap1 and Cul3 did not display any changes in their abundance, subcellular localization, or interaction in response to electrophilic stimuli. Our results demonstrate that the regulation of the Nrf2 protein level during stress responses is mediated by the activity but not the compn. of the Nrf2-Keap1-Cul3 complex.

      4
      Antioxidant response elements: Discovery, classes, regulation and potential applications
      Raghunath, Azhwar; Sundarraj, Kiruthika; Nagarajan, Raju; Arfuso, Frank; Bian, Jinsong; Kumar, Alan P.; Sethi, Gautam; Perumal, Ekambaram
      Redox Biology (2018), 17 (), 297-314CODEN: RBEIB3; ISSN:2213-2317. (Elsevier B.V.)
      A review. Exposure to antioxidants and xenobiotics triggers the expression of a myriad of genes encoding antioxidant proteins, detoxifying enzymes, and xenobiotic transporters to offer protection against oxidative stress. This articulated universal mechanism is regulated through the cis-acting elements in an array of Nrf2 target genes called antioxidant response elements (AREs), which play a crit. role in redox homeostasis. Though the Keap1/Nrf2/ARE system involves many players, AREs hold the key in transcriptional regulation of cytoprotective genes. ARE-mediated reporter constructs have been widely used, including xenobiotics profiling and Nrf2 activator screening. The complexity of AREs is brought by the presence of other regulatory elements within the AREs. The diversity in the ARE sequences not only bring regulatory selectivity of diverse transcription factors, but also confer functional complexity in the Keap1/Nrf2/ARE pathway. The different transcription factors either homodimerize or heterodimerize to bind the AREs. Depending on the nature of partners, they may activate or suppress the transcription. Attention is required for deeper mechanistic understanding of ARE-mediated gene regulation. The computational methods of identification and anal. of AREs are still in their infancy. Investigations are required to know whether epigenetics mechanism plays a role in the regulation of genes mediated through AREs. The polymorphisms in the AREs leading to oxidative stress related diseases are warranted. A thorough understanding of AREs will pave the way for the development of therapeutic agents against cancer, neurodegenerative, cardiovascular, metabolic and other diseases with oxidative stress.

      Environmental pollutant and potent mutagen 3-nitrobenzanthrone forms DNA adducts after reduction by NAD(P)H:quinone oxidoreductase and conjugation by acetyltransferases and sulfotransferases in human hepatic cytosols
      Arlt, Volker M.; Stiborova, Marie; Henderson, Colin J.; Osborne, Martin R.; Bieler, Christian A.; Frei, Eva; Martinek, Vaclav; Sopko, Bruno; Wolf, C. Roland; Schmeiser, Heinz H.; Phillips, David H.
      Cancer Research (2005), 65 (7), 2644-2652CODEN: CNREA8; ISSN:0008-5472. (American Association for Cancer Research)
      3-Nitrobenzanthrone (3-nitro-7H-benz[de]anthracen-7-one, 3-NBA) is a potent mutagen and suspected human carcinogen identified in diesel exhaust and air pollution. The authors compared the ability of human hepatic cytosolic samples to catalyze DNA adduct formation by 3-NBA. Using the 32P-postlabeling method, the authors found that 12/12 hepatic cytosols activated 3-NBA to form multiple DNA adducts similar to those formed in vivo in rodents. By comparing 3-NBA-DNA adduct formation in the presence of cofactors of NAD(P)H:quinone oxidoreductase (NQO1) and xanthine oxidase, most of the reductive activation of 3-NBA in human hepatic cytosols was attributed to NQO1. Inhibition of adduct formation by dicoumarol, an NQO1 inhibitor, supported this finding and was confirmed with human recombinant NQO1. When cofactors of N,O-acetyltransferases (NAT) and sulfotransferases (SULT) were added to cytosolic samples, 3-NBA-DNA adduct formation increased 10- to 35-fold. Using human recombinant NQO1 and NATs or SULTs, the authors found that mainly NAT2, followed by SULT1A2, NAT1, and, to a lesser extent, SULT1A1 activate 3-NBA. The authors also evaluated the role of hepatic NADPH:cytochrome P 450 oxidoreductase (POR) in the activation of 3-NBA in vivo by treating hepatic POR-null mice and wild-type littermates i.p. with 0.2 or 2 mg/kg body wt. of 3-NBA. No difference in DNA binding was found in any tissue examd. (liver, lung, kidney, bladder, and colon) between null and wild-type mice, indicating that 3-NBA is predominantly activated by cytosolic nitroreductases rather than microsomal POR. Collectively, these results show the role of human hepatic NQO1 to reduce 3-NBA to species being further activated by NATs and SULTs.

      The KEAP1-NRF2 system: a thiol-based sensor-effector apparatus for maintaining redox homeostasis
      Yamamoto, Masayuki; Kensler, Thomas W.; Motohashi, Hozumi
      Physiological Reviews (2018), 98 (3), 1169-1203CODEN: PHREA7; ISSN:1522-1210. (American Physiological Society)
      A review. The Kelch-like ECH-assocd. protein 1-NF-E2-related factor 2 (KEAP1-NRF2) system forms the major node of cellular and organismal defense against oxidative and electrophilic stresses of both exogenous and endogenous origins. KEAP1 acts as a cysteine thiol-rich sensor of redox insults, whereas NRF2 is a transcription factor that robustly transduces chem. signals to regulate a battery of cytoprotective genes. KEAP1 represses NRF2 activity under quiescent conditions, whereas NRF2 is liberated from KEAP1-mediated repression on exposure to stresses. The rapid inducibility of a response based on a derepression mechanism is an important feature of the KEAP1-NRF2 system. Recent studies have unveiled the complexities of the functional contributions of the KEAP1-NRF2 system and defined its broader involvement in biol. processes, including cell proliferation and differentiation, as well as cytoprotection. In this review, we describe historical milestones in the initial characterization of the KEAP1-NRF2 system and provide a comprehensive overview of the mol. mechanisms governing the functions of KEAP1 and NRF2, as well as their roles in physiol. and pathol. We also refer to the clin. significance of the KEAP1-NRF2 system as an important prophylactic and therapeutic target for various diseases, particularly aging-related disorders. We believe that controlled harnessing of the KEAP1-NRF2 system is a key to healthy aging and well-being in humans.

      Keap1 is a forked-stem dimer structure with two large spheres enclosing the intervening, double glycine repeat, and C-terminal domains
      Ogura, Toshihiko; Tong, Kit I.; Mio, Kazuhiro; Maruyama, Yuusuke; Kurokawa, Hirofumi; Sato, Chikara; Yamamoto, Masayuki
      Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences of the United States of America (2010), 107 (7), 2842-2847, S2842/1-S2842/3CODEN: PNASA6; ISSN:0027-8424. (National Academy of Sciences)
      Keap1 is a substrate adaptor of a Cullin 3-based E3 ubiquitin ligase complex that recognizes Nrf2, and also acts as a cellular sensor for xenobiotics and oxidative stresses. Nrf2 is a transcriptional factor regulating the expression of cytoprotective enzyme genes in response to such stresses. Under unstressed conditions Keap1 binds Nrf2 and results in rapid degrdn. of Nrf2 through the proteasome pathway. In contrast, upon exposure to oxidative and electrophilic stress, reactive cysteine residues in the IVR (intervening region) and BTB (Broad complex, Tramtrack, and Bric-a-Brac) domains of Keap1 are modified by electrophiles. This modification prevents Nrf2 from rapid degrdn. and induces Nrf2 activity by repression of Keap1. Here we report the structure of mouse Keap1 homodimer by single particle electron microscopy. Three-dimensional reconstruction at 24-Å resoln. revealed two large spheres attached by short linker arms to the sides of a small forked-stem structure, resembling a cherry-bob. Each sphere has a tunnel corresponding to the central hole of the β-propeller domain, as detd. by x-ray crystallog. The IVR domain appears to surround the core of the β-propeller domain. The unexpected proximity of IVR to the β-propeller domain suggests that any distortions generated during modification of reactive cysteine residues in the IVR domain may send a derepression signal to the p-propeller domain and thereby stabilize Nrf2. This study thus provides a structural basis for the two-site binding and hinge-latch model of stress sensing by the Nrf2-Keap1 system.

      Regulatory flexibility in the Nrf2-mediated stress response is conferred by conformational cycling of the Keap1-Nrf2 protein complex
      Baird, Liam; Lleres, David; Swift, Sam; Dinkova-Kostova, Albena T.
      Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences of the United States of America (2013), 110 (38), 15259-15264,S15259/1-S15259/8CODEN: PNASA6; ISSN:0027-8424. (National Academy of Sciences)
      The transcription factor NF-E2 p45-related factor 2 (Nrf2), a master regulator of cytoprotective genes, is controlled by dimeric Kelch-like ECH assocd. protein 1 (Keap1), a substrate adaptor protein for Cullin3/RING-box protein 1 ubiquitin ligase, which normally targets Nrf2 for ubiquitination and degrdn. but loses this ability in response to electrophiles and oxidants (inducers). By using recombinant proteins and populations of cells, some of the general features of the regulation of Nrf2 by Keap1 have been outlined. However, how the two proteins interact at a single-cell level is presently unknown. We now report the development of a quant. Forster resonance energy transfer-based system using multiphoton fluorescence lifetime imaging microscopy and its application for investigating the interaction between Nrf2 and Keap1 in single live cells. By using this approach, we found that under homeostatic conditions, the interaction between Keap1 and Nrf2 follows a cycle in which the complex sequentially adopts two distinct conformations: "open," in which Nrf2 interacts with a single mol. of Keap1, followed by "closed," in which Nrf2 binds to both members of the Keap1 dimer. Inducers disrupt this cycle by causing accumulation of the complex in the closed conformation without release of Nrf2. As a consequence, free Keap1 is not regenerated, and newly synthesized Nrf2 is stabilized. On the basis of these findings, we propose a model we have named the "cyclic sequential attachment and regeneration model of Keap1-mediated degrdn. of Nrf2.". This previously unanticipated dynamism allows rapid transcriptional responses to environmental changes and can accommodate multiple modes of regulation.

      Keap1, the cysteine-based mammalian intracellular sensor for electrophiles and oxidants
      Dinkova-Kostova, Albena T.; Kostov, Rumen V.; Canning, Peter
      Archives of Biochemistry and Biophysics (2017), 617 (), 84-93CODEN: ABBIA4; ISSN:0003-9861. (Elsevier B.V.)
      A review. The Kelch-like ECH assocd. protein 1 (Keap1) is a component of a Cullin3-based Cullin-RING E3 ubiquitin ligase (CRL) multisubunit protein complex. Within the CRL, homodimeric Keap1 functions as the Cullin3 adaptor, and importantly, it is also the crit. component of the E3 ligase that performs the substrate recognition. The best-characterized substrate of Keap1 is transcription factor NF-E2 p45-related factor 2 (Nrf2), which orchestrates an elaborate transcriptional program in response to environmental challenges caused by oxidants, electrophiles and pro-inflammatory agents, allowing adaptation and survival under stress conditions. Keap1 is equipped with reactive cysteine residues that act as sensors for endogenously produced and exogenously encountered small mols. (termed inducers), which have a characteristic chem. signature, reactivity with sulfhydryl groups. Inducers modify the cysteine sensors of Keap1 and impair its ability to target Nrf2 for ubiquitination and degrdn. Consequently, Nrf2 accumulates, enters the nucleus and drives the transcription of its target genes, which encode a large network of cytoprotective proteins. Here we summarize the early studies leading to the prediction of the existence of Keap1, followed by the discovery of Keap1 as the main neg. regulator of Nrf2. We then describe the available structural information on Keap1, its assembly with Cullin3, and its interaction with Nrf2. We also discuss the multiple cysteine sensors of Keap1 that allow for detection of a wide range of endogenous and environmental inducers, and provide fine-tuning and tight control of the Keap1/Nrf2 stress-sensing response.

      Monitoring Keap1-Nrf2 interactions in single live cells
      Baird, Liam; Swift, Sam; Lleres, David; Dinkova-Kostova, Albena T.
      Biotechnology Advances (2014), 32 (6), 1133-1144CODEN: BIADDD; ISSN:0734-9750. (Elsevier)
      The transcription factor NF-E2 p45-related factor 2 (Nrf2) and its neg. regulator Kelch-like ECH assocd. protein 1 (Keap1) control the expression of nearly 500 genes with diverse cytoprotective functions. Keap1, a substrate adaptor protein for Cullin3/Rbx1 ubiquitin ligase, normally continuously targets Nrf2 for degrdn., but loses this ability in response to electrophiles and oxidants (termed inducers). Consequently, Nrf2 accumulates and activates transcription of its downstream target genes. Many inducers are phytochems., and cruciferous vegetables represent one of the richest sources of inducer activity among the most commonly used edible plants. Here we summarize the discovery of the isothiocyanate sulforaphane as a potent inducer which reacts with cysteine sensors of Keap1, leading to activation of Nrf2. We then describe the development of a quant. Forster resonance energy transfer (FRET)-based methodol. combined with multiphoton fluorescence lifetime imaging microscopy (FLIM) to investigate the interactions between Keap1 and Nrf2 in single live cells, and the effect of sulforaphane, and other cysteine-reactive inducers, on the dynamics of the Keap1-Nrf2 protein complex. We present the exptl. evidence for the "cyclic sequential attachment and regeneration" or "conformation cycling" model of Keap1-mediated Nrf2 degrdn. Finally, we discuss the implications of this mode of regulation of Nrf2 for achieving a fine balance under normal physiol. conditions, and the consequences and mechanisms of disrupting this balance for tumor biol.

      Mechanisms of activation of the transcription factor Nrf2 by redox stressors, nutrient cues, and energy status and the pathways through which it attenuates degenerative disease
      Tebay, Lauren E.; Robertson, Holly; Durant, Stephen T.; Vitale, Steven R.; Penning, Trevor M.; Dinkova-Kostova, Albena T.; Hayes, John D.
      Free Radical Biology & Medicine (2015), 88 (Part_B), 108-146CODEN: FRBMEH; ISSN:0891-5849. (Elsevier B.V.)
      A review. Nuclear factor-erythroid 2 p45-related factor 2 (Nrf2) regulates the basal and stress-inducible expression of a battery of genes encoding key components of the glutathione-based and thioredoxin-based antioxidant systems, as well as aldo-keto reductase, glutathione S-transferase, and NAD(P)H:quinone oxidoreductase-1 drug-metabolizing isoenzymes along with multidrug-resistance-assocd. efflux pumps. It therefore plays a pivotal role in both intrinsic resistance and cellular adaptation to reactive oxygen species (ROS) and xenobiotics. Activation of Nrf2 can, however, serve as a double-edged sword because some of the genes it induces may contribute to chem. carcinogenesis by promoting futile redox cycling of polycyclic arom. hydrocarbon metabolites or confer resistance to chemotherapeutic drugs by increasing the expression of efflux pumps, suggesting its cytoprotective effects will vary in a context-specific fashion. In addn. to cytoprotection, Nrf2 also controls genes involved in intermediary metab., pos. regulating those involved in NADPH generation, purine biosynthesis, and the β-oxidn. of fatty acids, while suppressing those involved in lipogenesis and gluconeogenesis. Nrf2 is subject to regulation at multiple levels. Its ability to orchestrate adaptation to oxidants and electrophiles is due principally to stress-stimulated modification of thiols within one of its repressors, the Kelch-like ECH-assocd. protein 1 (Keap1), which is present in the cullin-3 RING ubiquitin ligase (CRL) complex CRLKeap1. Thus modification of Cys residues in Keap1 blocks CRLKeap1 activity, allowing newly translated Nrf2 to accumulate rapidly and induce its target genes. The ability of Keap1 to repress Nrf2 can be attenuated by p62/sequestosome-1 in a mechanistic target of rapamycin complex 1 (mTORC1)-dependent manner, thereby allowing refeeding after fasting to increase Nrf2-target gene expression. In parallel with repression by Keap1, Nrf2 is also repressed by β-transducin repeat-contg. protein (β-TrCP), present in the Skp1-cullin-1-F-box protein (SCF) ubiquitin ligase complex SCFβ-TrCP. The ability of SCFβ-TrCP to suppress Nrf2 activity is itself enhanced by prior phosphorylation of the transcription factor by glycogen synthase kinase-3 (GSK-3) through formation of a DSGIS-contg. phosphodegron. However, formation of the phosphodegron in Nrf2 by GSK-3 is inhibited by stimuli that activate protein kinase B (PKB)/Akt. In particular, PKB/Akt activity can be increased by phosphoinositide 3-kinase and mTORC2, thereby providing an explanation of why antioxidant-responsive element-driven genes are induced by growth factors and nutrients. Thus Nrf2 activity is tightly controlled via CRLKeap1 and SCFβ-TrCP by oxidative stress and energy-based signals, allowing it to mediate adaptive responses that restore redox homeostasis and modulate intermediary metab. Based on the fact that Nrf2 influences multiple biochem. pathways in both pos. and neg. ways, it is likely its dose-response curve, in terms of susceptibility to certain degenerative disease, is U-shaped. Specifically, too little Nrf2 activity will lead to loss of cytoprotection, diminished antioxidant capacity, and lowered β-oxidn. of fatty acids, while conversely also exhibiting heightened sensitivity to ROS-based signaling that involves receptor tyrosine kinases and apoptosis signal-regulating kinase-1. By contrast, too much Nrf2 activity disturbs the homeostatic balance in favor of redn., and so may have deleterious consequences including overprodn. of reduced glutathione and NADPH, the blunting of ROS-based signal transduction, epithelial cell hyperplasia, and failure of certain cell types to differentiate correctly. We discuss the basis of a putative U-shaped Nrf2 dose-response curve in terms of potentially competing processes relevant to different stages of tumorigenesis.

      Transcription factor NRF2 as a therapeutic target for chronic diseases: a systems medicine approach
      Cuadrado, Antonio; Manda, Gina; Hassan, Ahmed; Alcaraz, Maria Jose; Barbas, Coral; Daiber, Andreas; Ghezzi, Pietro; Leon, Rafael; Lopez, Manuela G.; Oliva, Baldo; Pajares, Marta; Rojo, Ana I.; Robledinos-Anton, Natalia; Valverde, Angela M.; Guney, Emre; Schmidt, Harald H. H. W.
      Pharmacological Reviews (2018), 70 (2), 348-383CODEN: PAREAQ; ISSN:1521-0081. (American Society for Pharmacology and Experimental Therapeutics)
      Systems medicine has a mechanism-based rather than a symptom- or organ-based approach to disease and identifies therapeutic targets in a nonhypothesis-driven manner. In this work, we apply this to transcription factor nuclear factor (erythroid-derived 2)-like 2 (NRF2) by cross-validating its position in a protein-protein interaction network (the NRF2 interactome) functionally linked to cytoprotection in low-grade stress, chronic inflammation, metabolic alterations, and reactive oxygen species formation. Multiscale network anal. of these mol. profiles suggests alterations of NRF2 expression and activity as a common mechanism in a subnetwork of diseases (the NRF2 diseasome). This network joins apparently heterogeneous phenotypes such as autoimmune, respiratory, digestive, cardiovascular, metabolic, and neurodegenerative diseases, along with cancer. Importantly, this approach matches and confirms in silico several applications for NRF2-modulating drugs validated in vivo at different phases of clin. development. Pharmacol., their profile is as diverse as electrophilic di-Me fumarate, synthetic triterpenoids like bardoxolone Me and sulforaphane, protein-protein or DNA-protein interaction inhibitors, and even registered drugs such as metformin and statins, which activate NRF2 and may be repurposed for indications within the NRF2 cluster of disease phenotypes. Thus, NRF2 represents one of the first targets fully embraced by classic and systems medicine approaches to facilitate both drug development and drug repurposing by focusing on a set of disease phenotypes that appear to be mechanistically linked. The resulting NRF2 drugome may therefore rapidly advance several surprising clin. options for this subset of chronic diseases.

      Small molecules inhibiting Keap1-Nrf2 protein-protein interactions: a novel approach to activate Nrf2 function
      Zhuang, Chunlin; Wu, Zhongli; Xing, Chengguo; Miao, Zhenyuan
      MedChemComm (2017), 8 (2), 286-294CODEN: MCCEAY; ISSN:2040-2503. (Royal Society of Chemistry)
      Oxidative stress is well recognized to contribute to the cause of a wide range of diseases, such as cancer, diabetes, Alzheimer's disease, arteriosclerosis, and inflammation. The Keap1-Nrf2-ARE pathway plays a crit. regulatory role and can protect cells from oxidative stress through activating Nrf2 to induce its downstream phase II enzymes. Nrf2 activation through the covalent inactivation of Keap1 may cause unpredictable side effects. Non-covalent disruption of the Keap1-Nrf2 protein-protein interactions is an alternative strategy for Nrf2 activation, potentially with reduced risk of toxicity. Efforts have been made in recent years to develop peptide- and small mol.-based Keap1-Nrf2 PPI inhibitors via different approaches, including high-throughput screening, target-based virtual screening, structure-based optimization, and fragment-based drug design. This review aims to highlight the recently discovered small-mol. inhibitors as well as their therapeutic potential.

      Non-covalent Small-Molecule Kelch-like ECH-Associated Protein 1-Nuclear Factor Erythroid 2-Related Factor 2 (Keap1-Nrf2) Inhibitors and Their Potential for Targeting Central Nervous System Diseases
      Pallesen, Jakob S.; Tran, Kim T.; Bach, Anders
      Journal of Medicinal Chemistry (2018), 61 (18), 8088-8103CODEN: JMCMAR; ISSN:0022-2623. (American Chemical Society)
      The transcription factor nuclear factor erythroid 2-related factor 2 (Nrf2) has a protective effect against oxidative stress and plays a major role in inflammation and central nervous system (CNS) diseases. Inhibition of the protein-protein interaction (PPI) between Nrf2 and its repressor protein, Kelch-like ECH-assocd. protein 1 (Keap1), leads to translocation of Nrf2 from the cytosol to the nucleus and expression of detoxifying antioxidant enzymes. To date, several non-covalent small-mol. Keap1-Nrf2 inhibitors have been identified; however, many of them contain carboxylic acids and are rather large in size, which likely prevents or decreases CNS permeability. This Perspective describes current small-mol. Keap1-Nrf2 inhibitors with exptl. evidence for the ability to inhibit the Keap1-Nrf2 interaction by binding to Keap1 in a non-covalent manner. Binding data, biostructural studies, and biol. activity are summarized for the inhibitors, and their potential as CNS tool compds. is discussed by analyzing physicochem. properties, including CNS multiparameter optimization (MPO) scoring algorithms. Finally, several strategies for identifying CNS-targeting Keap1 inhibitors are described.

      Kinetic, thermodynamic, and structural characterizations of the association between Nrf2-DLGex degron and Keap1
      Fukutomi, Toshiaki; Takagi, Kenji; Mizushima, Tsunehiro; Ohuchi, Noriaki; Yamamoto, Masayuki
      Molecular and Cellular Biology (2014), 34 (5), 832-846, 16 pp.CODEN: MCEBD4; ISSN:1098-5549. (American Society for Microbiology)
      Transcription factor Nrf2 (NF-E2-related factor 2) coordinately regulates cytoprotective gene expression, but under unstressed conditions, Nrf2 is degraded rapidly through Keap1 (Kelch-like ECH-assocd. protein 1)-mediated ubiquitination. Nrf2 harbors two Keap1-binding motifs, DLG and ETGE. Interactions between these two motifs and Keap1 constitute a key regulatory nexus for cellular Nrf2 activity through the formation of a two-site binding hinge-and-latch mechanism. In this study, we detd. the min. Keap1-binding sequence of the DLG motif, the low-affinity latch site, and defined a new DLGex motif that covers a sequence much longer than that previously defined. We have successfully clarified the crystal structure of the Keap1-DC-DLGex complex at 1.6 Å. DLGex possesses a complicated helix structure, which interprets well the human-cancer-derived loss-of-function mutations in DLGex. In thermodn. analyses, Keap1-DLGex binding is characterized as enthalpy and entropy driven, while Keap1-ETGE binding is characterized as purely enthalpy driven. In kinetic analyses, Keap1-DLGex binding follows a fast-assocn. and fast-dissocn. model, while Keap1-ETGE binding contains a slow-reaction step that leads to a stable conformation. These results demonstrate that the mode of DLGex binding to Keap1 is distinct from that of ETGE structurally, thermodynamically, and kinetically and support our contention that the DLGex motif serves as a converter transmitting environmental stress to Nrf2 induction as the latch site.

      Peptide inhibitors of the Keap1-Nrf2 protein-protein interaction
      Hancock, Rowena; Bertrand, Helene C.; Tsujita, Tadayuki; Naz, Shama; El-Bakry, Ayman; Laoruchupong, Jitnueng; Hayes, John D.; Wells, Geoff

      Disruption of the interaction between the ubiquitination facilitator protein Keap1 and the cap'n'collar basic-region leucine-zipper transcription factor Nrf2 is a potential strategy to enhance expression of antioxidant and free radical detoxification gene products regulated by Nrf2.       A systematic molecular dynamics approach to the study of peptide Keap1-Nrf2 protein-protein interaction inhibitors and its application to p62 peptides

      Protein-protein interactions (PPIs) as drug targets have been gaining growing interest, though developing drug-like small mol. PPI inhibitors remains challenging. Computational methods combining mol. dynamics simulations and binding energy calcns. could give both the structural and the energetic perspective of peptide PPI inhibitors. Our research results not only provide a useful method to investigate the Keap1-Nrf2 peptide inhibitors, but also give a good example to show how to incorporate computational methods into the study of peptide PPI inhibitors. Besides, applying this method to p62 peptides provides a detailed explanation for the expression of cytoprotective Nrf2 targets induced by p62 phosphorylation, which may benefit the further study of the crosstalk between the Keap1-Nrf2 pathway and p62-mediated selective autophagy.

      Binding thermodynamics and kinetics guided optimization of potent Keap1-Nrf2 peptide inhibitors

      Updated Research and Applications of Small Molecule Inhibitors of Keap1-Nrf2 Protein-Protein Interaction: a Review
      Zhuang, Chunlin; Miao, Zhenyuan; Sheng, Chunquan; Zhang, Wannian
      Current Medicinal Chemistry (2014), 21 (16), 1861-1870CODEN: CMCHE7; ISSN:0929-8673. (Bentham Science Publishers Ltd.)
      A review. The Keap1-Nrf2-ARE pathway is one of the most important regulators of cytoprotective responses to oxidative and/or electrophilic stresses, which is believed to play a crit. role in the development of many diseases, such as cancer, Alzheimer's, Parkinson's, and inflammatory bowel disease. Recent research indicates that the modulation of ARE activation via direct inhibition of the Keap1-Nrf2protein-protein interaction has many advantages, particularly the low cytotoxicity, over indirect covalent modulators of Keap1 protein for the discovery of novel small mol. modulators of this pathway. However, most known inducers (e.g., triterpenoids, isothiocyanates and sulfoxythiocarbamates) that activate the ARE system through electrophilic attacks on the cysteine sulfhydryl group of Keap1 also disrupt the Keap1-Nrf2 interaction. The understanding of co-crystal complex of the Keap1-Nrf2 interaction thus provides a structural basis for the rational design of highly potent direct inhibitors. This review summarizes the recent advances in the medicinal chem. of small-mol. inhibitors in the areas of drug design, structure-activity relationships, and biol. and biochem. properties. The peptides designed from DLG and ETGE motifs of Nrf2 protein that binds to Keap1 Kelch domain with promising binding affinities are highlighted. This review also includes recently reported non-peptide inhibitors with moderate inhibition by high-throughput screening. It is clear that further research is required for the discovery of more potent inhibitors.

      Pargyline reduces renal damage associated with ischaemia-reperfusion and cyclosporin
      Chaaya, Rana; Alfarano, Chiara; Guilbeau-Frugier, Celine; Coatrieux, Christelle; Kesteman, Anne-Sylvie; Parini, Angelo; Fares, Nassim; Gue, Michelle; Schanstra, Joost P.; Bascands, Jean-Loup
      Nephrology, Dialysis, Transplantation (2011), 26 (2), 489-498CODEN: NDTREA; ISSN:0931-0509. (Oxford University Press)
      Slow deterioration of the kidney graft is characterized histol. by interstitial fibrosis and tubular atrophy (IFTA). Immunol. and non-immunol. stress is the main cause of progression towards IFTA. Our study focused on the non-immunol. injuries induced by ischemia-reperfusion (IR) and cyclosporin (CsA) toxicity, which remain the two stress factors putting a damper on the outcome of the renal graft. Endogenous reactive oxygen species (ROS) are essentially produced by mitochondria, and we have previously shown that the blockage of the mitochondrial enzymes monoamine oxidases (MAOs) prevents H2O2 prodn. in the early reperfusion stage following IR. We used a rat model of IFTA consisting in unilateral nephrectomy followed by IR and daily CsA administration. 4 Wk after IR, we analyzed renal function, histol. alterations and a no. of inflammatory and fibrotic genes. We obsd., 28 days after pargyline-mediated blockade of MAO (before or after IR), improved renal function as well as a net decrease in renal inflammation assocd. to lower IL-1β and TNF-α gene expression. However, significant redn. in apoptosis, necrosis and fibrosis was only obsd. when pargyline was administrated before IR. This protective effect was assocd. to decreased expression of TGF-β1, collagen types I, III and IV and also to the normalization of antioxidant (SOD1, catalase) and inflammatory (COX2, LOX5) gene expression. It appears that the blockage of ROS produced by MAO and subsequent cell death might be an effective protective strategy against IFTA progression.

      Discovery of a Kelch-like ECH-associated protein 1-inhibitory tetrapeptide and its structural characterization
      Sogabe, Satoshi; Sakamoto, Kotaro; Kamada, Yusuke; Kadotani, Akito; Fukuda, Yasunori; Sakamoto, Jun-ichi

Although the tetrapeptide's affinity is not very high, our results may help facilitate the designing of small-mol. inhibitors during lead generation in drug discovery.

      Discovery of a small-molecule inhibitor and cellular probe of Keap1-Nrf2 protein-protein interaction
      Hu, Longqin; Magesh, Sadagopan; Chen, Lin; Wang, Lili; Lewis, Timothy A.; Chen, Yu; Khodier, Carol; Inoyama, Daigo; Beamer, Lesa J.; Emge, Thomas J.; Shen, Jian; Kerrigan, John E.; Kong, Ah-Ng Tony; Dandapani, Sivaraman; Palmer, Michelle; Schreiber, Stuart L.; Munoz, Benito

      Characterization of RA839, a Noncovalent Small Molecule Binder to Keap1 and Selective Activator of Nrf2 Signaling
      Winkel, Angelika F.; Engel, Christian K.; Margerie, Daniel; Kannt, Aimo; Szillat, Hauke; Glombik, Heiner; Kallus, Christopher; Ruf, Sven; Guessregen, Stefan; Riedel, Jens; Herling, Andreas W.; von Knethen, Andreas; Weigert, Andreas; Bruene, Bernhard; Schmoll, Dieter

      The activation of the transcription factor NF-E2-related factor 2 (Nrf2) maintains cellular homeostasis in response to oxidative stress by the regulation of multiple cytoprotective genes. These pathways were also activated after the activation of Nrf2 by the silencing of Keap1 expression. RA839 regulated only two genes in Nrf2 knock-out macrophages.       Fragment-growing guided design of Keap1-Nrf2 protein-protein interaction inhibitors for targeting myocarditis
      Meng, Ning; Tang, Hua; Zhang, Hao; Jiang, Chengshi; Su, Li; Min, Xiao; Zhang, Wannian; Zhang, Hua; Miao, Zhenyuan; Zhang, Wen; Zhuang, Chunlin
      Free Radical Biology & Medicine (2018), 117 (), 228-237CODEN: FRBMEH; ISSN:0891-5849. (Elsevier B.V.)
      Small-mol. inhibitors that block the Keap1-Nrf2 protein-protein interactions are being intensely pursued as a new therapeutic strategy for oxidative stress-related diseases, such as cancer, diabetes, Alzheimer's disease, arteriosclerosis, inflammation and myocarditis. However, there are not enough studies on antioxidant treatments using small mols. in myocarditis. The authors herein provided a series of novel hydronaphthoquinones as the Keap1-Nrf2 interaction inhibitors targeting LPS-induced myocarditis both in vitro and in vivo. These compds. were designed through an in-silico fragment growing approach based on the previous reported compd. The new compds. were predicted to form addnl. hydrogen bonds with the S363 residue, leading to higher inhibitory activity. Among these new derivs., compds. These inhibitors can dose-dependently protect the H9c2 cardiac cells against LPS-induced injury (100% at 2 μM and 4 μM) and effectively prolong survival or save the life of LPS-injured mice. Mechanistic studies showed that these inhibitors could release Nrf2 in H9c2 cells and LPS-inflammatory mouse models and translocate into the nucleus in a dose-response manner, which significantly increased the downstream genes (HO-1, NQO-1) and the pro-inflammatory cytokines (TNF-α, IL-1β, IL-6), while ROS prodn. dramatically decreased. Their protective effects and the mechanism of action were further confirmed by siNrf2 transfected expt. Collectively, the novel hydronaphthoquinones can be used as promising lead compds. for the study of Keap1-Nrf2 protein-protein interactions and further antimyocarditis drug development.

      Polar Recognition Group Study of Keap1-Nrf2 Protein-Protein Interaction Inhibitors
      Lu, Meng-Chen; Tan, Shi-Jie; Ji, Jian-Ai; Chen, Zhi-Yun; Yuan, Zhen-Wei; You, Qi-Dong; Jiang, Zheng-Yu
      ACS Medicinal Chemistry Letters (2016), 7 (9), 835-840CODEN: AMCLCT; ISSN:1948-5875. (American Chemical Society)
      Directly disrupting the Keap1-Nrf2 protein-protein interaction (PPI) has emerged as an attractive way to activate Nrf2, and Keap1-Nrf2 PPI inhibitors have been proposed as potential agents to relieve inflammatory and oxidative stress diseases.       Discovery of Potent Keap1-Nrf2 Protein-Protein Interaction Inhibitor Based on Molecular Binding Determinants Analysis
      Jiang, Zheng-Yu; Lu, Meng-Chen; Xu, Li-Li; Yang, Ting-Ting; Xi, Mei-Yang; Xu, Xiao-Li; Guo, Xiao-Ke; Zhang, Xiao-Jin; You, Qi-Dong; Sun, Hao-Peng
      Journal of Medicinal Chemistry (2014), 57 (6), 2736-2745CODEN: JMCMAR; ISSN:0022-2623. (American Chemical Society)
      Keap1 is known to mediate the ubiquitination of Nrf2, a master regulator of the antioxidant response. Directly interrupting the Keap1-Nrf2 interaction has been emerged as a promising strategy to develop novel class of antioxidant, antiinflammatory, and anticancer agents. On the basis of the mol. binding determinants anal. of Keap1, we successfully designed and characterized the most potent protein-protein interaction (PPI) inhibitor of Keap1-Nrf2, compd. 2, with KD value of 3.59 nM binding to Keap1 for the first time to single-digit nanomolar. Compd. 2 can effectively disrupt the Nrf2-Keap1 interaction with an EC50 of 28.6 nM in the fluorescence polarization assay. The qRT-PCR results of Nrf2 transcription targets gave the consistent results. These results confirm direct and highly efficient interruption of the Keap1-Nrf2 PPI can be fully achieved by small mols.

      Structure-Activity and Structure-Conformation Relationships of Aryl Propionic Acid Inhibitors of the Kelch-like ECH-Associated Protein 1/Nuclear Factor Erythroid 2-Related Factor 2 (KEAP1/NRF2) Protein-Protein Interaction
      Heightman, Tom D.; Callahan, James F.; Chiarparin, Elisabetta; Coyle, Joseph E.; Griffiths-Jones, Charlotte; Lakdawala, Ami S.; McMenamin, Rachel; Mortenson, Paul N.; Norton, David; Peakman, Torren M.; Rich, Sharna J.; Richardson, Caroline; Rumsey, William L.; Sanchez, Yolanda; Saxty, Gordon; Willems, Henriette M.       Lu, Meng-Chen; Ji, Jian-Ai; Jiang, Yong-Lin; Chen, Zhi-Yun; Yuan, Zhen-Wei; You, Qi-Dong; Jiang, Zheng-Yu

These results suggested that activation of Nrf2 by directly inhibiting the Keap1-Nrf2 PPI may be beneficial as a treatment for UC.

      Monitoring drug target engagement in cells and tissues using the cellular thermal shift assay
      Martinez Molina Daniel; Jafari Rozbeh; Ignatushchenko Marina; Seki Takahiro; Larsson E Andreas; Dan Chen; Sreekumar Lekshmy; Cao Yihai; Nordlund Par
      Science (New York, N.Y.) (2013), 341 (6141), 84-7 ISSN:.
      The efficacy of therapeutics is dependent on a drug binding to its cognate target. Optimization of target engagement by drugs in cells is often challenging, because drug binding cannot be monitored inside cells. We have developed a method for evaluating drug binding to target proteins in cells and tissue samples. This cellular thermal shift assay (CETSA) is based on the biophysical principle of ligand-induced thermal stabilization of target proteins. Using this assay, we validated drug binding for a set of important clinical targets and monitored processes of drug transport and activation, off-target effects and drug resistance in cancer cell lines, as well as drug distribution in tissues. CETSA is likely to become a valuable tool for the validation and optimization of drug target engagement.

      A perspective on the epidemiology of acetaminophen exposure and toxicity in the United States
      Blieden, Marissa; Paramore, L. Clark; Shah, Dhvani; Ben-Joseph, Rami
      Expert Review of Clinical Pharmacology (2014), 7 (3), 341-348CODEN: ERCPAG; ISSN:1751-2433. (Informa Healthcare)
      A review. Acetaminophen is a commonly-used analgesic in the US and, at doses of more than 4 g/day, can lead to serious hepatotoxicity. Recent FDA and CMS decisions serve to limit and monitor exposure to high-dose acetaminophen. This literature review aims to describe the exposure to and consequences of high-dose acetaminophen among chronic pain patients in the US. Each year in the US, approx. 6% of adults are prescribed acetaminophen doses of more than 4 g/day and 30,000 patients are hospitalized for acetaminophen toxicity. Up to half of acetaminophen overdoses are unintentional, largely related to opioid-acetaminophen combinations and attempts to achieve better symptom relief. Liver injury occurs in 17% of adults with unintentional acetaminophen overdose.
